<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41477"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Fern genomes elucidate land plant evolution and cyanobacterial symbioses"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Plants 2018 4:7"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2018-07-02"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2018 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Ferns are the closest sister group to all seed plants, yet little is known about their genomes other than that they are generally colossal. Here, we report on the genomes of Azolla filiculoides and Salvinia cucullata (Salviniales) and present evidence for episodic whole-genome duplication in ferns&#8212;one at the base of &#8216;core leptosporangiates&#8217; and one specific to Azolla. One fern-specific gene that we identified, recently shown to confer high insect resistance, seems to have been derived from bacteria through horizontal gene transfer. Azolla coexists in a unique symbiosis with N2-fixing cyanobacteria, and we demonstrate a clear pattern of cospeciation between the two partners. Furthermore, the Azolla genome lacks genes that are common to arbuscular mycorrhizal and root nodule symbioses, and we identify several putative transporter genes specific to Azolla&#8211;cyanobacterial symbiosis. These genomic resources will help in exploring the biotechnological potential of Azolla and address fundamental questions in the evolution of plant life. The genomes of two fern species, Azolla filiculoides and Salvinia cucullata, are reported and provide insights into fern-specific whole-genome duplications, fern-specific insect-resistant gene evolution and fern&#8211;cyanobacterial symbiosis."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Ferns are the closest sister group to all seed plants, yet little is known about their genomes other than that they are generally colossal. Here, we report on the genomes of <i>Azolla filiculoides</i> and <i>Salvinia cucullata</i> (Salviniales) and present evidence for episodic whole-genome duplication in ferns—one at the base of ‘core leptosporangiates’ and one specific to <i>Azolla</i>. One fern-specific gene that we identified, recently shown to confer high insect resistance, seems to have been derived from bacteria through horizontal gene transfer. <i>Azolla</i> coexists in a unique symbiosis with N<sub>2</sub>-fixing cyanobacteria, and we demonstrate a clear pattern of cospeciation between the two partners. Furthermore, the <i>Azolla</i> genome lacks genes that are common to arbuscular mycorrhizal and root nodule symbioses, and we identify several putative transporter genes specific to <i>Azolla</i>–cyanobacterial symbiosis. These genomic resources will help in exploring the biotechnological potential of <i>Azolla</i> and address fundamental questions in the evolution of plant life.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The advent of land plants ~474–515 Myr ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Morris, J. L. et al. The timescale of early land plant evolution. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 115, E2274–E2283 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d52717e1379">1</a></sup> led to complex vegetational innovations that shaped emerging terrestrial and freshwater ecosystems. Bryophytes, lycophytes, ferns and gymnosperms dominated the global landscape before the ecological radiation of flowering plants 90 Myr ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Schneider, H. et al. Ferns diversified in the shadow of angiosperms. Nature 428, 553–557 (2004)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d52717e1383">2</a></sup>. The first complete plant genome sequence (<i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>) was published in 2000<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="The Arabidopsis Genome Initiative Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 796–815 (2000)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d52717e1390">3</a></sup>, followed by reference genomes for all other major lineages of green plants, except ferns. A dearth of genomic information from this entire lineage has limited not only our knowledge of fern biology but also the processes that govern the evolution of land plants.</p><p>The relatively small genome (0.75 Gb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Obermayer, R., Leitch, I. J., Hanson, L. &amp; Bennett, M. D. Nuclear DNA C-values in 30 species double the familial representation in pteridophytes. Ann. Bot. 90, 209–217 (2002)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d52717e1397">4</a></sup> of <i>Azolla</i> is exceptional among ferns, a group that is notorious for genomes as large as 148 Gb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Hidalgo, O. et al. Is there an upper limit to genome size? Trends Plant Sci. 22, 567–573 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d52717e1404">5</a></sup> and averaging 12 Gb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Sessa, E. B. &amp; Der, J. P. Evolutionary genomics of ferns and lycophytes. Adv. Bot. Res. 78, 215–254 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d52717e1408">6</a></sup>. <i>Azolla</i> is one of the fastest-growing plants on the planet, with demonstrated potential to be a significant carbon sink. Data from the Arctic Ocean show that, ~50 Myr ago, in middle-Eocene sediments, an abundance of fossilized <i>Azolla</i> characterizes an ~800,000-year interval known as the ‘<i>Azolla</i> event’<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Brinkhuis, H. et al. Episodic fresh surface waters in the Eocene Arctic Ocean. Nature 441, 606–609 (2006)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d52717e1422">7</a></sup>. This period coincides with the shift from the early Eocene greenhouse world towards our present icehouse climate, suggesting that <i>Azolla</i> had a role in abrupt global cooling by sequestering atmospheric carbon dioxide<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Speelman, E. N. et al. The Eocene Arctic Azolla bloom: environmental conditions, productivity and carbon drawdown. Geobiology 7, 155–170 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d52717e1429">8</a></sup>. <i>Azolla</i> is also remarkable in harbouring an obligate, N<sub>2</sub>-fixing cyanobacterium, <i>Nostoc azollae</i>, within specialized leaf cavities. Because of this capability, <i>Azolla</i> has been used as ‘green manure’ for over 1,000 years to bolster rice productivity in Southeast Asia<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Lumpkin, T. A. &amp; Plucknett, D. L. Azolla: botany, physiology, and use as a green manure. Econ. Bot. 34, 111–153 (1980)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d52717e1445">9</a></sup>. The <i>Azolla</i> symbiosis is unique among plant–bacterial endosymbiotic associations because the cyanobiont is associated with the fern throughout its life cycle, being vertically transmitted during sexual reproduction to subsequent generations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Zheng, W. et al. Cellular responses in the cyanobacterial symbiont during its vertical transfer between plant generations in the Azolla microphylla symbiosis. New Phytol. 181, 53–61 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d52717e1452">10</a></sup>. In all other land plant symbiotic associations, the relationship must be renewed each generation. The <i>Nostoc</i> symbiont is not capable of autonomous growth and its genome shows clear signs of reduction, with several housekeeping genes lost or pseudogenized<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Ran, L. et al. Genome erosion in a nitrogen-fixing vertically transmitted endosymbiotic multicellular cyanobacterium. PLoS ONE 5, e11486 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d52717e1460">11</a></sup>. With a fossil record that extends back to the mid-Cretaceous period, <i>Azolla</i> probably shares a ~100-Myr-old co-evolutionary relationship with <i>Nostoc</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Hall, J. W. &amp; Swanson, N. P. Studies on fossil Azolla: Azolla montana, a Cretaceous megaspore with many small floats. Am. J. Bot. 55, 1055–1061 (1968)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d52717e1469">12</a></sup>.</p><p>To better understand genome size evolution in <i>Azolla</i> and its closely related lineages, we obtained genome size estimates for all five genera of Salviniales (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM1">1</a>). We found them to be at least an order of magnitude smaller than any other fern species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#Fig1">1a</a>), and, most notably, the genome of <i>Salvinia cucullata</i>, which belongs to the sister genus to <i>Azolla</i>, is only 0.26 Gb, the smallest genome size ever reported in ferns. This unanticipated discovery afforded us the opportunity to include a second fern genome for comparison.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Genome size evolution in Salviniales."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Genome size evolution in Salviniales.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0188-8/MediaObjects/41477_2018_188_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0188-8/MediaObjects/41477_2018_188_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="766" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Members of Salviniales have smaller genome sizes than other ferns (averaging 1C = 12 Gb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Sessa, E. B. &amp; Der, J. P. Evolutionary genomics of ferns and lycophytes. Adv. Bot. Res. 78, 215–254 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d52717e1506">6</a></sup>. Two whole-genome duplication (WGD) events identified in this study were mapped onto the phylogeny, with divergence time estimates obtained from Testo and Sundue<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 129" title="Testo, W. &amp; Sundue, M. A 4000-species dataset provides new insight into the evolution of ferns. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 105, 200–211 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR129" id="ref-link-section-d52717e1510">129</a></sup>. <b>b</b>,<b>c</b>, Whole genomes were assembled from <i>A.</i> <i>filiculoides</i> (<b>b</b>) and <i>S.</i> <i>cucullata</i> (<b>c</b>). <b>d</b>,<b>e</b>, The genome of <i>S.</i> <i>cucullata</i> has substantially reduced levels of RNA (<b>d</b>) and DNA (<b>e</b>) transposons compared to <i>A.</i> <i>filiculoides</i>. Image in panel <b>c</b> courtesy of P.-F. Lu.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome assembly and annotation</h3><p>To gain insight into fern genome evolution, as well as plant–cyanobacterial symbioses, we sequenced the genomes of <i>A.</i> <i>filiculoides</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#Fig1">1b</a>) and <i>S.</i> <i>cucullata</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#Fig1">1c</a>) using Illumina and PacBio technologies. The assembled <i>Azolla</i> and <i>Salvinia</i> genomes have N50 contig size of 964.7 Kb and 719.8 Kb, respectively. The BUSCO (Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Orthologs) assessment and Illumina read-mapping results indicate high completeness for both assemblies (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM1">1</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM1">2</a>). We identified 20,201 and 19,914 high-confidence gene models in <i>Azolla</i> and <i>Salvinia</i>, respectively, that are supported by transcript evidence or had significant similarity to other known plant proteins (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM1">1</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM1">3</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM1">3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM1">Supplementary Discussion</a>). <i>Salvinia</i> genes are much more compact, with a mean intron length half of that in <i>Azolla</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM1">1</a>). In addition to introns, differences in the repetitive content explain some of the nearly threefold difference in genome size. <i>Azolla</i> has more of every major category of repeat, but 191 Mb of the 233-Mb difference in the total repetitive content are made of retroelements, especially Gypsy and Copia long terminal repeat retrotransposons (LTR-RTs; Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#Fig1">1d</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM1">4</a>). DNA transposon profiles are similar for the two ferns except that <i>Azolla</i> has substantially more SOLA elements than does <i>Salvinia</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#Fig1">1e</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Insights into gene family evolution in land plants</h3><p>The genomes of <i>Azolla</i> and <i>Salvinia</i> offer a new opportunity to examine the evolution of plant genes and gene families across all Viridiplantae (land plants plus green algae). We classified genes into orthogroups from 23 genomes (12 angiosperms, 2 gymnosperms, 2 ferns, 1 lycophyte, 2 mosses, 2 liverworts, 1 charophyte and 1 chlorophyte; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM3">5</a>) and reconstructed the gene family evolution—gain, loss, expansion and contraction—across the green tree of life (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM1">5</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM3">5</a>). To investigate the origin of genes linked to seed development, we examined orthogroups containing 48 transcription factors that express exclusively in <i>Arabidopsis</i> seeds<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Le, B. H. et al. Global analysis of gene activity during Arabidopsis seed development and identification of seed-specific transcription factors. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 107, 8063–8070 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d52717e1694">13</a></sup>. Homologues of 39 of them were detected in ferns or other seed-free plants, indicating that many seed transcription factors were present before the origin of seeds (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM4">6</a>). Similarly, only a handful of transcription factor families arose along the branch that led to seed plants (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM5">7</a>); rather than relying on entirely novel transcription factors, it seems instead that an expansion of pre-existing transcription factor families had a greater role in seed plant evolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Wilhelmsson, P. K. I., Mühlich, C., Ullrich, K. K. &amp; Rensing, S. A. Comprehensive genome-wide classification reveals that many plant-specific transcription factors evolved in streptophyte algae. Genome Biol. Evol. 9, 3384–3397 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d52717e1704">14</a></sup>. Indeed, ancestral gene number reconstructions of MADS-intervening keratin-like and C-terminal (MIKC)-type MADS box genes (orthogroup 23) show that these important developmental regulators more than doubled in number from 15 in the ancestral vascular plant to 31 in the ancestral euphyllophyte (here, Salviniales plus seed plants; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM3">5</a>).</p><p>In a recent study on the evolution of plant transcription-associated proteins, which include transcription factors and transcriptional regulators<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Wilhelmsson, P. K. I., Mühlich, C., Ullrich, K. K. &amp; Rensing, S. A. Comprehensive genome-wide classification reveals that many plant-specific transcription factors evolved in streptophyte algae. Genome Biol. Evol. 9, 3384–3397 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d52717e1714">14</a></sup>, ferns were exclusively represented by the <i>Pteridium aquilinum</i> transcriptome. The finding that the transcriptional regulator Polycomb group EZ (PcG_EZ) was lost in ferns is corroborated here by our whole-genome data (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM6">8</a>). Conversely, the transcription factor ULTRAPETALA, which originated at the base of euphyllophytes and is present in <i>P.</i> <i>aquilinum</i>, was apparently secondarily lost in Salviniales (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM6">8</a>). YABBY, an important transcription factor that patterns leaf polarity in flowering plants, is absent in our fern genomes and in the genome of the lycophyte <i>Selaginella moellendorffii</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Banks, J. A. et al. The Selaginella genome identifies genetic changes associated with the evolution of vascular plants. Science 332, 960–963 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d52717e1736">15</a></sup> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM6">8</a>). Interestingly, a <i>YABBY</i> homologue was recently identified in a separate lycophyte species—<i>Huperzia selago</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Evkaikina, A. I. et al. The Huperzia selago shoot tip transcriptome sheds new light on the evolution of leaves. Genome Biol. Evol. 9, 2444–2460 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d52717e1748">16</a></sup>—suggesting that <i>YABBY</i> has been lost at least twice in land plant evolution (in <i>Selaginella</i> and in ferns). How the differential retention of <i>YABBY</i> shaped the evolution of the vascular plant body plan requires further studies.</p><p>Among the orthogroups specific to seed plants, 1-aminocyclopropane-1-carboxylic acid (ACC) oxidase is of special interest because it converts ACC to ethylene—the last step in the ethylene biosynthetic pathway (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#Fig2">2</a>). Ethylene is a critical plant hormone that controls various important physiological responses (for example, fruit ripening, flowering time, seed germination and internode elongation). Because ethylene responses are known in bryophytes, lycophytes and ferns<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Van de Poel, B., Cooper, E. D., Delwiche, C. F. &amp; Chang, C. in Ethylene in Plants (ed. Wen, C. K.) 109–134 (Springer, Dordrecht, 2014).." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d52717e1768">17</a></sup>, it is intriguing to find that ACC oxidase only evolved within seed plants, a result confirming that seed-free plants must possess an alternative ethylene-forming mechanism<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Osborne, D. J., Walters, J., Milborrow, B. V., Norville, A. &amp; Stange, L. M. C. Evidence for a non-ACC ethylene biosynthesis pathway in lower plants. Phytochemistry 42, 51–60 (1996)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d52717e1772">18</a></sup>. Two other mechanisms, found in bacteria and fungi, result in ethylene formation: one via the 2-oxoglutarate-dependent ethylene-forming enzyme and the other through the non-enzymatic conversion of 2-keto-4-methylthiobutyric acid (KMBA) into ethylene<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Van de Poel, B., Cooper, E. D., Delwiche, C. F. &amp; Chang, C. in Ethylene in Plants (ed. Wen, C. K.) 109–134 (Springer, Dordrecht, 2014).." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d52717e1776">17</a></sup>. We did not identify ethylene-forming enzyme in seed-free plant genomes, suggesting the absence of the ethylene-forming enzyme-based biosynthetic pathway. Seed-free plants may possibly synthesize ethylene non-enzymatically via KMBA; however, further biochemical studies are needed to test this hypothesis. Interestingly, ACC synthase (upstream of ACC oxidase) is present in seed-free plants, albeit in a lower copy number (&lt;3) compared to seed plants, which average 9.3 copies (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#Fig2">2</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM1">6</a>). Paralogues of ACC synthase in seed plants are differentially regulated in response to varying developmental or environmental stimuli<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Tsuchisaka, A. et al. A combinatorial interplay among the 1-aminocyclopropane-1-carboxylate isoforms regulates ethylene biosynthesis in Arabidopsis thaliana. Genetics 183, 979–1003 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d52717e1787">19</a></sup>. Thus, it is plausible that the expansion of the ACC synthase family was coupled with the origin of ACC oxidase in seed plants to create a regulated ethylene biosynthetic pathway.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Evolution of ethylene biosynthesis."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Evolution of ethylene biosynthesis.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0188-8/MediaObjects/41477_2018_188_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0188-8/MediaObjects/41477_2018_188_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="486" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>The ethylene-forming pathway involves the Yang cycle, where ACC is synthesized from <i>S</i>-adenosyl-methionine (SAM; also known as AdoMet) by ACC synthase. ACC oxidase then catalyses the conversion of ACC to ethylene. We found that ACC oxidase is unique to seed plants (green) and its origin probably drove the expansion of the ACC synthase gene family (orange; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM1">6</a>) to create a regulated ethylene biosynthetic mechanism.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">The history of whole-genome duplication in ferns</h3><p>Our Multi-tAxon Paleopolyploidy Search (MAPS)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Li, Z. et al. Early genome duplications in conifers and other seed plants. Sci. Adv. 1, e1501084 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d52717e1825">20</a></sup> phylogenomic analyses of the <i>Azolla</i> and <i>Salvinia</i> genomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#Fig3">3a</a>), together with all available transcriptome data from other ferns, support two whole-genome duplication (WGD) events: a recent WGD event occurring in <i>Azolla</i> following its divergence from <i>Salvinia</i> and an earlier WGD predating the origin of ‘core leptosporangiates’ (sensu Pryer at al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Pryer, K. M. et al. Phylogeny and evolution of ferns (monilophytes) with a focus on the early leptosporangiate divergences. Am. J. Bot. 91, 1582–1598 (2004)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d52717e1845">21</a></sup>), a large clade comprising the heterosporous, tree and polypod ferns. The observed peaks of duplication associated with the inferred WGDs exceeded the 95% confidence intervals of our birth and death simulations for gene family evolution in the absence of WGDs. This high number of shared gene duplications is readily explained by a significant episodic birth event, such as a WGD. The discovery that <i>Azolla</i> experienced a genome duplication independent of other heterosporous ferns is not entirely surprising because <i>Azolla</i> has nearly twice the number of chromosomes of other heterosporous ferns, including <i>Salvinia</i> and <i>Pilularia</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Rice, A. et al. The Chromosome Counts Database (CCDB)—a community resource of plant chromosome numbers. New Phytol. 206, 19–26 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d52717e1861">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Wood, T. E. et al. The frequency of polyploid speciation in vascular plants. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 106, 13875–13879 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d52717e1864">23</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#Fig1">1a</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="The history of WGD in Azolla and Salvinia."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: The history of WGD in <i>Azolla</i> and <i>Salvinia</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0188-8/MediaObjects/41477_2018_188_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0188-8/MediaObjects/41477_2018_188_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="549" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b>, MAPS analysis identified two WGD events: one specific to <i>Azolla</i> (orange circle) and one predating the core leptosporangiates (green circle). The blue line illustrates the percentage of subtrees indicative of a gene duplication shared by the descendants at each node. The grey lines display the gene birth–death simulation results without WGD. The species divergence dates are from Testo and Sundue<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 129" title="Testo, W. &amp; Sundue, M. A 4000-species dataset provides new insight into the evolution of ferns. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 105, 200–211 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR129" id="ref-link-section-d52717e1895">129</a></sup>. <b>b</b>, Density plots from fitting Gaussian mixture models to <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> distributions estimated from pairs of syntenic paralogues within the <i>Azolla</i> and <i>Salvinia</i> genomes, as well as of syntenic orthologues between <i>Azolla</i> and <i>Salvinia</i>. <b>c</b>, Examples of synteny between <i>Azolla</i> and <i>Salvinia</i> genomic regions. The left and right panels display a 2:1 and 2:2 syntenic relationship between <i>Azolla</i> and <i>Salvinia</i> regions, respectively. Each subpanel represents a genomic region in <i>Azolla</i> or <i>Salvinia</i>, with gene models on both strands shown above and below the dashed line. High-scoring sequence pairs (HSPs) in protein-coding sequences are marked by short vertical bars above the gene models. Selected HSP links between genomic regions are depicted as coloured lines crossing the subpanels, whereas others (for example, the HSP links between the two <i>Azolla</i> genomic regions in the left panel) are left out for clarity. Collinear series of HSPs across genomic regions indicates a syntenic relationship between the regions concerned. Genomic regions conserved in duplicate after the WGD that occurred prior to the divergence between <i>Azolla</i> and <i>Salvinia</i> should show a 2:2 syntenic relationship, whereas regions conserved in duplicate after the <i>Azolla</i>-specific WGD should show a 2:1 syntenic relationship with <i>Salvinia</i> regions. The left and right panels can be regenerated at <a href="https://genomevolution.org/r/ujll">https://genomevolution.org/r/ujll</a> and <a href="https://genomevolution.org/r/ukys">https://genomevolution.org/r/ukys</a>, respectively. </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To further substantiate the two WGD events identified by MAPS, we examined the distribution of synonymous distances (<i>K</i><sub>s</sub>) between syntenic paralogues within each of the genomes, as well as syntenic orthologues between <i>Azolla</i> and <i>Salvinia</i>. In the <i>Azolla</i> genome, we detected 242 syntenic blocks comprising 988 syntelog pairs. By contrast, only 83 syntenic blocks with 254 syntelog pairs could be found in <i>Salvinia</i>. Between <i>Azolla</i> and <i>Salvinia</i>, 3,587 pairs of syntenic orthologues were detected, clustering into 356 syntenic genomic blocks. We fit Gaussian mixture models to identify peaks in the <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> distributions (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#Fig3">3b</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM1">7</a>). The main peak for <i>Azolla</i>–<i>Salvinia</i> orthologue pairs centres at ~1.0, which marks the species divergence between the two genera. To the left of this peak is the major <i>Azolla</i> intragenomic peak (~0.8), whose position confirms the <i>Azolla</i>-specific WGD event (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#Fig3">3b</a>). To the right of the <i>Azolla</i>–<i>Salvinia</i> divergence peak is the <i>Salvinia</i> intragenomic <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> peak (~1.2–1.3), which matches a minor <i>Azolla</i> intragenomic peak, consistent with the proposed pre-core leptosporangiates WGD (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#Fig3">3b</a>). Moreover, despite the antiquity of the WGDs and species divergence (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#Fig1">1a</a>), we were still able to detect <i>Azolla</i>–<i>Salvinia</i> syntenic regions in a 2:1 or 2:2 syntenic relationship (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#Fig3">3c</a>), respectively, corroborating the <i>Azolla</i>-specific and the older WGD events. The confirmation of these two WGDs in ferns further allows us to characterize patterns of gene retention following WGD. We found that <i>Azolla</i> syntenic paralogues are enriched for transcription-related genes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM7">9</a>), similar to what was observed in <i>Arabidopsis</i> and other angiosperms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Maere, S. et al. Modeling gene and genome duplications in eukaryotes. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 102, 5454–5459 (2005)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2077">24</a></sup>. Likewise, protein kinases, another functional category commonly retained after WGD in seed plants, are significantly enriched in <i>Salvinia</i> syntenic paralogues (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM7">9</a>). Additional genomic data are needed to better characterize the distribution of WGD events across the fern tree of life and to compare patterns of post-WGD gene fractionation with those documented in seed plants.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">The pentatricopeptide repeat family and RNA editing</h3><p>The pentatricopeptide repeat (PPR) family is the largest gene family found in the <i>Azolla</i> and <i>Salvinia</i> genomes, with the <i>Azolla</i> genome encoding over 2,000 PPR proteins and the <i>Salvinia</i> genome over 1,700 PPR proteins. PPRs are implicated in organellar RNA processing<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Barkan, A. &amp; Small, I. Pentatricopeptide repeat proteins in plants. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 65, 415–442 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2107">25</a></sup>, and the large repertoire of PPRs correlates well with the extensive RNA editing we observed in the organellar genomes of Salviniales: 1,710 sites in <i>Azolla</i> organelles and 1,221 sites in <i>Salvinia</i> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM8">10</a>). These editing events include both C-to-U conversions (~70%) and U-to-C conversions (~30%). The number of PPR genes and the degree of RNA editing greatly exceed that found in seed plants and most bryophytes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Takenaka, M., Zehrmann, A., Verbitskiy, D., Härtel, B. &amp; Brennicke, A. RNA editing in plants and its evolution. Annu. Rev. Genet. 47, 335–352 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2121">26</a></sup>. Of the sequenced plant genomes, only that of <i>S.</i> <i>moellendorffii</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Banks, J. A. et al. The Selaginella genome identifies genetic changes associated with the evolution of vascular plants. Science 332, 960–963 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2131">15</a></sup> has more PPR genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Cheng, S. et al. Redefining the structural motifs that determine RNA binding and RNA editing by pentatricopeptide repeat proteins in land plants. Plant J. 85, 532–547 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2135">27</a></sup>, correlating with the hyperediting seen in lycophytes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Oldenkott, B., Yamaguchi, K., Tsuji-Tsukinoki, S., Knie, N. &amp; Knoop, V. Chloroplast RNA editing going extreme: more than 3400 events of C-to-U editing in the chloroplast transcriptome of the lycophyte Selaginella uncinata. RNA 20, 1499–1506 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2139">28</a></sup>. However, there are no U-to-C editing events in <i>Selaginella</i>, making the <i>Azolla</i> and <i>Salvinia</i> genome sequences a novel and valuable resource for identifying the unknown factors catalysing these events.</p><p>More than half of the plastid transcripts and two-thirds of the mitochondrial transcripts in <i>Azolla</i> and <i>Salvinia</i> require start codon creation by C-to-U editing or stop codon removal by U-to-C editing before translation is possible. Most stop codon edits (76%) and start codon edits (62%) are shared between <i>Azolla</i> and <i>Salvinia</i> plastomes (as opposed to only 19% in internal ACG codons; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM1">10</a>). This persistence of start and stop codon edits suggests that their loss is selected against, that is, creating the translatable sequence by RNA editing has an advantage over having it encoded by the genome. This argues that these particular RNA-editing events are not selectively neutral<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Gray, M. W. Evolutionary origin of RNA editing. Biochemistry 51, 5235–5242 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2171">29</a></sup> and supports editing as a control mechanism for gene expression in fern organelles.</p><p>Only ~55–60% of PPR proteins (1,220 in <i>Azolla</i> and 930 in <i>Salvinia</i>) contain domains associated with RNA editing in other plants. Although sufficient to account for the number of editing events observed (assuming each protein can specify one or a few sites as in other plants), this leaves a very large number of PPR proteins (~700 in <i>Azolla</i> and ~600 in <i>Salvinia</i>) with unknown functions. By comparison, flowering plants contain only 200–250 PPR proteins that lack editing domains.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Origin and evolution of a fern insecticidal protein</h3><p>Ferns are remarkable for their high levels of insect resistance compared to flowering plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Hendrix, S. D. An evolutionary and ecological perspective of the insect fauna of ferns. Am. Nat. 115, 171–196 (1980)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2199">30</a></sup>. Recently, Shukla et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Shukla, A. K. et al. Expression of an insecticidal fern protein in cotton protects against whitefly. Nat. Biotechnol. 34, 1046–1051 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2203">31</a></sup> isolated a novel insecticidal protein, Tma12, from the fern <i>Tectaria macrodonta</i>. Transgenic cottons carrying <i>Tma12</i> exhibit outstanding resistance to whitefly, yet show no decrease in yields, demonstrating tremendous agricultural potential. Tma12 has a high similarity to chitin-binding proteins (Pfam PF03067), but its evolutionary origin is unknown. Here, we found a <i>Tma12</i> homologue to be present in the <i>Salvinia</i> genome (henceforth <i>ScTma12</i>), as well as in a few 1,000 Plants (1KP)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Matasci, N. et al. Data access for the 1,000 Plants (1KP) project. GigaScience 3, 1–10 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2223">32</a></sup> fern transcriptomes, but not in <i>Azolla</i> or any other publicly available plant genomes. Phylogenetic analyses position the fern <i>Tma12</i> sequences together with bacterial sequences, and are most closely related to the chitin-binding proteins from Chloroflexi (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#Fig4">4</a>). We investigated whether this insecticidal protein was more likely a result of horizontal gene transfer (HGT) from bacteria to ferns or produced by fern-associated microorganisms. <i>ScTma12</i> is in a 646,687-bp scaffold (Sacu_v1.1_s0099) and has an 247-bp intron. The genes upstream and downstream of <i>ScTma12</i> are all clearly plant genes, and we found no abnormality in read-mapping quality, nor an abrupt change in read coverage (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM1">9</a>), which together speak against the sequence being a contamination from a bacterial source. It has been argued that differential loss of genes in eukaryotes is the rule and gene acquisition by HGT rather rare<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Ku, C. et al. Endosymbiotic origin and differential loss of eukaryotic genes. Nature 524, 427–432 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2246">33</a></sup>. The concerted loss of <i>Tma12</i> in each of the other Viridiplantae lineages is unlikely but cannot entirely be ruled out. However, functional HGT into eukaryotes does occur<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Li, F.-W. et al. Horizontal transfer of an adaptive chimeric photoreceptor from bryophytes to ferns. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 111, 6672–6677 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2253">34</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Husnik, F. &amp; McCutcheon, J. P. Functional horizontal gene transfer from bacteria to eukaryotes. Nat. Rev. Microbiol. 16, 67–79 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2256">35</a></sup> and <i>ScTma12</i> might represent such a case that contributed to the well-documented resistance of ferns against phytophagous insects.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Origin of a fern insecticidal protein."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Origin of a fern insecticidal protein.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0188-8/MediaObjects/41477_2018_188_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0188-8/MediaObjects/41477_2018_188_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="539" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Phylogenetic analysis of the chitin-binding domain Pfam PF03067 shows that the fern Tma12 insecticidal protein was probably derived from bacteria through an ancient HGT event. The numbers above the branches are bootstrap (BS) support values (BS = 100 is omitted), and the thickened branches indicate BS &gt; 70. The tree is rooted based on the result from a broader phylogenetic analysis of PF03067 and PF08329 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM1">Supplementary Data</a>). The pink star denotes the sequence from the <i>S.</i> <i>cucullata</i> genome.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">
                           <i>Azolla</i>–cyanobacterial symbiosis</h3><p>To explore the co-evolutionary history of the <i>Azolla</i>–<i>Nostoc</i> symbiosis, we resequenced five other <i>Azolla</i> species and assembled each of their cyanobiont genomes. We then compared the cyanobiont phylogeny to the host species phylogeny and found a clear cospeciation pattern, with just one exception (the placement of <i>Azolla caroliniana</i>; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#Fig5">5a</a>). Although such a pattern has been hinted at before<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Van Coppenolle, B., McCouch, S. R., Watanabe, I., Huang, N. &amp; Van Hove, C. Genetic diversity and phylogeny analysis of Anabaena azollae based on RFLPs detected in Azolla–Anabaena azollae DNA complexes using nif gene probes. Theor. Appl. Genet. 91, 589–597 (1995)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2321">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Zheng, W. W., Nilsson, M., Bergman, B. &amp; Rasmussen, U. Genetic diversity and classification of cyanobacteria in different Azolla species by the use of PCR fingerprinting. Theor. Appl. Genet. 99, 1187–1193 (1999)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2324">37</a></sup>, we provide unequivocal evidence from whole-genome data. The genetic basis for this persistent symbiosis is undetermined. In plants, two other mutualistic associations—the arbuscular mycorrhizal (AM) and the nitrogen-fixing root nodule (RN) symbioses—have been well characterized. Whereas the AM symbiosis is formed between almost all land plants and a single fungal clade (Glomeromycota)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Parniske, M. Arbuscular mycorrhiza: the mother of plant root endosymbioses. Nat. Rev. Microbiol. 6, 763–775 (2008)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2328">38</a></sup>, the RN symbiosis is restricted to a few angiosperm lineages (mostly legumes) that associate with various nitrogen-fixing bacterial symbionts (for example, <i>Rhizobium</i> and <i>Frankia</i>). Despite these distinct differences, both symbioses require that a common symbiosis pathway (CSP) be established<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Parniske, M. Arbuscular mycorrhiza: the mother of plant root endosymbioses. Nat. Rev. Microbiol. 6, 763–775 (2008)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2338">38</a></sup>. This pathway is highly conserved in all land plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Delaux, P.-M. et al. Algal ancestor of land plants was preadapted for symbiosis. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 112, 13390–13395 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2342">39</a></sup>, except for those that have lost the AM symbiosis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Delaux, P.-M. et al. Comparative phylogenomics uncovers the impact of symbiotic associations on host genome evolution. PLoS Genet. 10, e1004487 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2347">40</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Bravo, A., York, T., Pumplin, N., Mueller, L. A. &amp; Harrison, M. J. Genes conserved for arbuscular mycorrhizal symbiosis identified through phylogenomics. Nat. Plants 2, 15208 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2350">41</a></sup>, such as <i>A.</i> <i>thaliana</i> and three aquatic angiosperms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Delaux, P.-M. et al. Comparative phylogenomics uncovers the impact of symbiotic associations on host genome evolution. PLoS Genet. 10, e1004487 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2360">40</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Bravo, A., York, T., Pumplin, N., Mueller, L. A. &amp; Harrison, M. J. Genes conserved for arbuscular mycorrhizal symbiosis identified through phylogenomics. Nat. Plants 2, 15208 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2363">41</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Azolla–cyanobacterial symbiosis."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: <i>Azolla</i>–cyanobacterial symbiosis.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0188-8/MediaObjects/41477_2018_188_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0188-8/MediaObjects/41477_2018_188_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="560" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p><b>a</b>, The cyanobiont phylogeny largely mirrors the host species phylogeny, indicating a convincing cospeciation pattern between the two partners. All nodes received a maximum likelihood bootstrap support of 100%, and for the host phylogeny, all nodes also received a local posterior probability of 1.0 from the ASTRAL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 119" title="Zhang, C., Sayyari, E. &amp; Mirarab, S. ASTRAL-III: polynomial time species tree reconstruction from partially resolved gene trees. BMC Evol. Biol. 19, 153 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR119" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2384">119</a></sup> analysis. Both the nuclear and the plastome data sets gave the same topology for the host, and the branch lengths shown here were from the plastome tree. Scale bars represent 0.01 substitutions per site. <b>b</b>, The CSP genes were lost in the <i>Azolla</i> and <i>Salvinia</i> genomes (empty boxes), whereas orthologues can be found in other fern transcriptomes (red boxes). *<i>Arabidopsis</i> lacks the CSP genes and does not have AM symbiosis. <b>c</b>, Cyanobionts have a large effect on the <i>Azolla</i> transcriptome. <b>d</b>, The <i>Azolla</i> transcriptome responds to nitrogen starvation more significantly when cyanobionts are absent than when they are present. PC, principal component. <b>e</b>, Candidate genes involved in nutrient transport and communication with cyanobionts.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We investigated whether the CSP might have been co-opted during the evolution of the <i>Azolla</i>–<i>Nostoc</i> symbiosis by searching for six essential CSP genes in the <i>Azolla</i> and <i>Salvinia</i> genomes, as well as in transcriptomic data from other ferns in the 1KP data set<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Matasci, N. et al. Data access for the 1,000 Plants (1KP) project. GigaScience 3, 1–10 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2439">32</a></sup> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM9">11</a>). Although <i>DMI2</i> (also known as <i>SYMRK</i>), <i>DMI3</i> (also known as <i>CCaMK</i>), <i>IPD3</i> (also known as <i>CYCLOPS</i>) and <i>VAPYRIN</i> were found in other ferns, the <i>Azolla</i> and <i>Salvinia</i> genomes completely lacked orthologues (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#Fig5">5b</a>). <i>IPD3</i> and <i>VAPYRIN</i> do not belong to multigene families<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Delaux, P.-M. et al. Algal ancestor of land plants was preadapted for symbiosis. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 112, 13390–13395 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2485">39</a></sup> and homologues were not detected. Although homologues of <i>DMI2</i> and <i>DMI3</i> were identified, phylogenetic analyses confirmed that they are not orthologous to the symbiotic genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM1">Supplementary Data</a>). In addition, for <i>DMI3</i>, we searched the <i>Azolla</i> and <i>Salvinia</i> homologues for two motifs (threonine 271 and the calmodulin-binding domain) that are critical for symbiosis. Both motifs are missing from these sequences, confirming the absence of <i>DMI3</i>. <i>CASTOR</i> and <i>POLLUX</i> are paralogues resulting from a gene duplication event in the ancestor of seed plants, and although pre-duplicated homologues are present in <i>Salvinia</i> and other seed-free plants, they are absent in <i>Azolla</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#Fig5">5b</a>). The co-elimination of the CSP genes suggests the lack of AM symbiosis in <i>Azolla</i> and <i>Salvinia</i> and that the nitrogen-fixing <i>Azolla</i>–<i>Nostoc</i> symbiosis does not rely on this pathway.</p><p>To identify genes important for the <i>Azolla</i>–<i>Nostoc</i> symbiosis, we treated <i>A.</i> <i>filiculoides</i> with erythromycin to remove the cyanobiont (AzCy−) and compared its gene expression patterns with the wild type (AzCy+). Experiments were carried out in conditions where the nitrogen nutrient (ammonium nitrate) was either supplied (N+) or withheld (N−) from the growth media. Results from <i>nifH</i> real-time PCR confirmed the complete absence of cyanobacteria in AzCy− and showed that the addition of the nitrogen nutrient suppresses symbiotic N<sub>2</sub> fixation in AzCy+ (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM1">10</a>), consistent with an earlier study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Brouwer, P. et al. Metabolic adaptation, a specialized leaf organ structure and vascular responses to diurnal N2 fixation by Nostoc azollae sustain the astonishing productivity of Azolla ferns without nitrogen fertilizer. Front. Plant Sci. 8, 442 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2563">42</a></sup>. A large portion of the transcriptome is affected by the presence or absence of cyanobionts, with 6,210 and 2,125 genes being differentially transcribed under N− and N+ conditions, respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#Fig5">5c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM1">Supplementary Discussion</a>). Of these, over 33% have at least a twofold expression difference. In response to nitrogen starvation, the <i>Azolla</i> transcriptomes remained moderately stable when the cyanobiont was present, but shifted drastically once it was absent (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#Fig5">5d</a>). This finding suggests that the presence of the cyanobiont buffers the transcriptomic profile of <i>Azolla</i> from fluctuations in environmental nitrogen availability.</p><p>We focused primarily on those genes that are differentially expressed between the nitrogen treatments when the cyanobiont is present, and to a lesser extent on when the cyanobiont is absent (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#Fig5">5e</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM1">Supplementary Discussion</a>). Because the cyanobacterial N<sub>2</sub>-fixation rate is strongly induced in the N− condition, we expect these genes to be candidates involved in nutrient exchange or in communication with the cyanobiont to promote N<sub>2</sub> fixation. A total of 88 upregulated and 72 downregulated genes were identified (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#Fig5">5e</a>). Among the upregulated genes is a paralogue of the ammonium transporter 2 subfamily (<i>AfAMT2-4</i>; Azfi_s0034.g025227; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#Fig5">5e</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM1">11</a>) that is probably dedicated to ammonium uptake from the <i>Azolla</i> leaf cavity where the cyanobiont resides; homologous ammonium transporters have been implicated to participate in the AM and RN symbioses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Breuillin-Sessoms, F. et al. Suppression of arbuscule degeneration in Medicago truncatula phosphate transporter4 mutants is dependent on the ammonium transporter 2 family protein AMT2;3. Plant Cell 27, 1352–1366 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2612">43</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="D’Apuzzo, E. et al. Characterization of three functional high-affinity ammonium transporters in Lotus japonicus with differential transcriptional regulation and spatial expression. Plant Physiol. 134, 1763–1774 (2004)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2615">44</a></sup>. There is also a paralogue of the molybdate transporter gene family (<i>AfMOT1</i>; Azfi_s0167.g054529) that is most likely specialized for supplying molybdenum, a required co-factor for nitrogenase, to the cyanobiont. One of the legume <i>MOT1</i> genes was recently found to facilitate nitrogenase activity in RN symbiosis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Tejada-Jiménez, M. et al. Medicago truncatula molybdate transporter type 1 (MtMOT1.3) is a plasma membrane molybdenum transporter required for nitrogenase activity in root nodules under molybdenum deficiency. New Phytol. 216, 1223–1235 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2626">45</a></sup>. In addition to these two transporters, we identified a chalcone synthase paralogue in this candidate gene set. Chalcone synthase catalyses the production of naringenin chalcone and is the first committed step in flavonoid biosynthesis. Interestingly, naringenin and naringin both have significant effects on promoting cyanobacterial growth<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Żyszka, B., Anioł, M. &amp; Lipok, J. Modulation of the growth and metabolic response of cyanobacteria by the multifaceted activity of naringenin. PLoS ONE 12, e0177631 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2630">46</a></sup> and differentiation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Cohen, M. F. &amp; Yamasaki, H. Flavonoid-induced expression of a symbiosis-related gene in the cyanobacterium Nostoc punctiforme. J. Bacteriol. 182, 4644–4646 (2000)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2634">47</a></sup>. Naringin is also a hormogonium-repressing factor<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Cohen, M. F. &amp; Yamasaki, H. Flavonoid-induced expression of a symbiosis-related gene in the cyanobacterium Nostoc punctiforme. J. Bacteriol. 182, 4644–4646 (2000)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2638">47</a></sup>. Because hormogonia lack heterocysts and cannot fix nitrogen, naringin (or related flavonoids) could act as a plant signal to boost N<sub>2</sub> fixation in the cyanobiont (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM1">Supplementary Discussion</a>).</p><p>Although the ancient and intimate nature of the <i>Azolla</i>–<i>Nostoc</i> relationship suggests that gene transfer from <i>Nostoc</i> to the <i>Azolla</i> nuclear genome may have occurred over time, a thorough homology search found no evidence of <i>Nostoc</i>-to-<i>Azolla</i> HGT. However, we did discover a cyanobacteria-derived gene in the <i>Azolla</i> genome, but one that is shared with other ferns. This gene encodes a squalene–hopene cyclase (SHC), which mediates the cyclization of squalene into hopene, and is thought to be the evolutionary progenitor of many classes of eukaryotic and prokaryotic sterol cyclases. We found SHC homologues in both the <i>Azolla</i> and the <i>Salvinia</i> genomes, as well as in 40 fern 1KP transcriptomes. Our reconstructed gene phylogeny clearly shows that the fern SHCs are nested among cyanobacteria sequences (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM1">12</a>). Although no homologue can be found in seed plants or in green algae, the SHC is also present in bryophyte (moss and liverwort) genomes and transcriptomes. Interestingly, these bryophyte SHCs are not related to those of ferns but are embedded in other bacterial SHC lineages (the monophyly of land plant SHCs is rejected by the Swofford–Olsen–Waddell–Hillis test<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Church, S. H., Ryan, J. F. &amp; Dunn, C. W. Automation and evaluation of the SOWH test with SOWHAT. Syst. Biol. 64, 1048–1058 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2683">48</a></sup>, <i>P</i> &lt; 0.005). This finding implies a complex evolutionary history for SHCs in land plants, possibly featuring independent transfers of SHC from different prokaryotic lineages to mosses, liverworts and ferns. We are confident that these SHC genes are not from contaminants because the gene phylogeny largely mirrors the species phylogeny; furthermore, the SHC genes were not assembled into stray scaffolds in the genomes of <i>Azolla</i>, <i>Salvinia</i>, <i>Physcomitrella</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Rensing, S. A. et al. The Physcomitrella genome reveals evolutionary insights into the conquest of land by plants. Science 319, 64–69 (2008)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2699">49</a></sup> or <i>Marchantia</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Bowman, J. L. et al. Insights into land plant evolution garnered from the Marchantia polymorpha genome. Cell 171, 287–304 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2705">50</a></sup>. In addition, we detected the triterpene products of SHC, hop-22(29)-ene, diplopterol and tetrahymanol, in <i>S.</i> <i>cucullata</i> biomass, providing direct evidence for SHC activity in this fern (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM1">13</a>). Similar observations of SHC-synthesized triterpenes have been made in polypod ferns<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Ageta, H., Iwata, K. &amp; Natori, S. A fern constituent, fernene, a triterpenoid hydrocarbon of a new type. Tetrahedron Lett. 22, 1447–1450 (1963)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2719">51</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Shinozaki, J., Shibuya, M., Masuda, K. &amp; Ebizuka, Y. Squalene cyclase and oxidosqualene cyclase from a fern. FEBS Lett. 582, 310–318 (2008)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2722">52</a></sup> and mosses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Marsili, A. &amp; Morelli, I. Triterpenes from mosses. Phytochemistry 7, 1705–1706 (1968)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2726">53</a></sup>. Because hopenes have an important role in plasma membrane stability in prokaryotes (similar to steroids in eukaryotes) and have been shown to confer low-temperature adaptation and stress tolerance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Sáenz, J. P. et al. Hopanoids as functional analogues of cholesterol in bacterial membranes. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 112, 11971–11976 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2730">54</a></sup>, it is plausible that the convergent evolution of hopene biosynthesis in seed-free plants, through independent HGTs from bacteria, might have contributed to the early adaptations of land plants to diverse and adverse environments. Functional studies are needed to confirm this hypothesis.</p><p>We anticipate that the availability of the first genomic data from ferns will continue to lead to vital insights into the processes that govern the evolution of plant genes and gene families. The implementation of fern data into the existing comparative genomic framework will enhance our understanding the plant tree of life.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Flow cytometry and genome size estimation</h3><p>To estimate the genome sizes of <i>S.</i> <i>cucullata</i>, <i>P.</i> <i>americana</i>, <i>Regnellidium diphyllum</i> and <i>Marsilea minuta</i> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM1">1</a>), we used the Beckman chopping buffer to extract nuclei from fresh leaves, following the protocol of Kuo and Huang<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Kuo, L.-Y. &amp; Huang, Y.-M. Determining genome size from spores of seedless vascular plants. Bio Protoc. 7, e2322 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2772">55</a></sup>. The nuclei extractions were mixed with those from standards, stained with 1/50 volume of propidium iodide solution (2.04 mg ml<sup>−1</sup>) and incubated at 4 °C in darkness for 1 h. For each species, three replicates were performed on the BD FACSCan system. For <i>S.</i> <i>cucullata</i>, we used <i>A.</i> <i>thaliana</i> (0.165 pg per C)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Praça-Fontes, M.
