M., Carvalho, C. R. &amp; Clarindo, W. R. C-value reassessment of plant standards: an image cytometry approach. Plant Cell Rep. 30, 2303–2312 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2791">56</a></sup> as the standard, and for all other samples, we used <i>Zea mays</i> ‘CE-777’ (2.785 pg per C)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Praça-Fontes, M. M., Carvalho, C. R. &amp; Clarindo, W. R. C-value reassessment of plant standards: an image cytometry approach. Plant Cell Rep. 30, 2303–2312 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2798">56</a></sup>. For each peak (in both standard and sample), over 1,000 nuclei were collected with cross-validation values lower than 5%, except for those of <i>A.</i> <i>thaliana</i> 2<i>n</i> nuclei peaks, which ranged from 5.5% to 5.9%. To calculate the 2C-value of <i>S.</i> <i>cucullata</i>, we used a formula of: (0.66 pg × (<i>F</i> − S2<i>n</i>) + 0.33 pg × (S4<i>n</i> − <i>F</i>))/(S4<i>n</i> − S2<i>n</i>). For all other samples, we used: 5.57 pg × <i>F</i>/S2<i>n</i>, where 0.66, 0.33 and 5.57 pg are the 4C-value of <i>A.</i> <i>thaliana</i>, the 2C-value of <i>A.</i> <i>thaliana</i> and the 2C-value of <i>Z.</i> <i>mays</i> ‘CE-777’, respectively. S2<i>n</i>, S4<i>n</i> and <i>F</i> are the relative fluorescence amount (that is, the peak mean value) of the standard 2<i>n</i> nuclei, standard 4<i>n</i> nuclei and the sample 2<i>n</i> nuclei, respectively.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec11">Genome and transcriptome sequencing</h3><p><i>A.</i> <i>filiculoides</i> was collected from the Galgenwaard ditch in Utrecht, the Netherlands, and propagated directly or sterilized as described in Dijkhuizen et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Dijkhuizen, L. W. et al. Is there foul play in the leaf pocket? The metagenome of floating fern Azolla reveals endophytes that do not fix N2 but may denitrify. New Phytol. 217, 453–466 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2894">57</a></sup>. <i>A.</i> <i>filiculoides</i> (sterilized without cyanobiont) DNA was extracted, then sequenced on PacBio RSII at 51× coverage<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Dijkhuizen, L. W. et al. Is there foul play in the leaf pocket? The metagenome of floating fern Azolla reveals endophytes that do not fix N2 but may denitrify. New Phytol. 217, 453–466 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2904">57</a></sup> and Illumina HiSeq2000 (100 bp paired-end; ~86× coverage; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM10">12</a>) with library insert sizes of 175 bp and 340 bp. RNA sequencing (RNA-seq) data from <i>A.</i> <i>filiculoides</i> of the Galgenwaard ditch used for annotation included the following RNA profiles: (1) at four time points during the diel cycle of fern sporophytes grown with or without 2 mM ammonium nitrate for 1 week<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Brouwer, P. et al. Metabolic adaptation, a specialized leaf organ structure and vascular responses to diurnal N2 fixation by Nostoc azollae sustain the astonishing productivity of Azolla ferns without nitrogen fertilizer. Front. Plant Sci. 8, 442 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2918">42</a></sup>; (2) of different reproductive stages comparing fern sporophytes, microsporocarps and megasporocarps collected at noon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Brouwer, P. et al. Azolla domestication towards a biobased economy? New Phytol. 202, 1069–1082 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2922">58</a></sup>; (3) of roots treated with cytokinin, indole-3-acetic acid (IAA) or none<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="de Vries, J. et al. Cytokinin-induced promotion of root meristem size in the fern Azolla supports a shoot-like origin of euphyllophyte roots. New Phytol. 209, 705–720 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2926">59</a></sup>; and (4) of sporophytes with or without cyanobacterial symbionts grown with or without ammonium nitrate for 2 weeks then collected at noon. Plant materials of <i>A.</i> <i>caroliniana</i>, <i>Azolla mexicana</i>, <i>Azolla microphylla</i>, <i>Azolla nilotica</i> and <i>Azolla rubra</i> were obtained from the International Rice Research Institute (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM1">1</a>) and DNA was extracted by a modified cetyltrimethylammonium bromide (CTAB) procedure<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Beck, J. B. et al. Does hybridization drive the transition to asexuality in diploid Boechera? Evolution 66, 985–995 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2953">60</a></sup>. Illumina libraries with a 500-bp insert size were prepared and sequenced on Illumina HiSeq2000 (100 bp paired-end; ~50× coverage; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM10">12</a>).</p><p><i>S.</i> <i>cucullata</i> was originally collected from Bangladesh and subsequently cultured at Taiwan Forestry Research Institute, Dr. Cecilia Koo Botanic Conservation Center and Duke University (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM1">1</a>). Genomic DNA was purified using a modified CTAB procedure<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Beck, J. B. et al. Does hybridization drive the transition to asexuality in diploid Boechera? Evolution 66, 985–995 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d52717e2971">60</a></sup> and sequenced on both PacBio RSII (10 SMRT cells; 46× coverage) and Illumina HiSeq2000 platforms (1 lane of 125 bp paired-end; 215× coverage; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM10">12</a>). <i>S.</i> <i>cucullata</i> RNA from the floating and submerged leaves was separately extracted using the Sigma Spectrum Plant Total RNA kit, each with three biological replicates. To examine patterns of RNA editing, one library per leaf type was prepared by the Illumina Ribozero Plant kit (that is, not poly-A enriched), whereas the other two were done by the Kapa Stranded mRNA-seq kit. These six RNA libraries were pooled and sequenced in one lane of Illumina HiSeq2000 (125 bp paired-end).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec12">Genome assembly</h3><p>We assembled the PacBio reads from <i>A.</i> <i>filiculoides</i> and <i>S.</i> <i>cucullata</i> genomes using PBcR<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Berlin, K. et al. Assembling large genomes with single-molecule sequencing and locality-sensitive hashing. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 623–630 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3005">61</a></sup>, and the resulting drafts were then polished by Quiver<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Chin, C.-S. et al. Nonhybrid, finished microbial genome assemblies from long-read SMRT sequencing data. Nat. Methods 10, 563–569 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3010">62</a></sup> (<i>A.</i> <i>filiculoides</i>) or Pilon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS ONE 9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3020">63</a></sup> (<i>S.</i> <i>cucullata</i>). Plastid genomes were separately assembled using Mitobim<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Hahn, C., Bachmann, L. &amp; Chevreux, B. Reconstructing mitochondrial genomes directly from genomic next-generation sequencing reads—a baiting and iterative mapping approach. Nucleic Acids Res. 41, e129 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3031">64</a></sup> and annotated in Geneious<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Kearse, M. et al. Geneious Basic: an integrated and extendable desktop software platform for the organization and analysis of sequence data. Bioinformatics 28, 1647–1649 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3035">65</a></sup> with manual adjustments. The PBcR contigs were filtered to remove plastome fragments. Although the <i>A.</i> <i>filiculoides</i> strain we sequenced was surface sterilized and treated with antibiotics to remove its associated microbiome, other endophytes could still persist, as shown by Dijkhuizen et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Dijkhuizen, L. W. et al. Is there foul play in the leaf pocket? The metagenome of floating fern Azolla reveals endophytes that do not fix N2 but may denitrify. New Phytol. 217, 453–466 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3045">57</a></sup>. Thus, we thoroughly assessed the <i>A.</i> <i>filiculoides</i> and <i>S.</i> <i>cucullata</i> assemblies to filter out all possible non-plant scaffolds. We used BlobTools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Laetsch, D. R. &amp; Blaxter, M. L. BlobTools: interrogation of genome assemblies. F1000Res. 6, 1287 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3062">66</a></sup> in combination with SILVA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Quast, C. et al. The SILVA ribosomal RNA gene database project: improved data processing and web-based tools. Nucleic Acids Res. 41, D590–D596 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3066">67</a></sup> and UniProt<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="The UniProt Consortium UniProt: the universal protein knowledgebase. Nucleic Acids Res. 45, D158–D169 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3070">68</a></sup> databases to infer the taxonomy for each scaffold. We removed all scaffolds that were classified as bacteria or fungi and also those that had a skewed GC content and read coverage. The completeness of each final assembly was assessed by BUSCO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Simão, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210–3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3075">69</a></sup> with the Plants set, and by using BWA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows–Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754–1760 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3079">70</a></sup> and HISAT2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Kim, D., Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. HISAT: a fast spliced aligner with low memory requirements. Nat. Methods 12, 357–360 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3083">71</a></sup> to map Illumina reads to the assemblies (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM1">2</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec13">Repeat annotation</h3><p>RepeatModeler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Smit, A. &amp; Hubley, R. RepeatModeler Open 1.0 (Institute for Systems Biology, 2015); &#xA;                    http://www.repeatmasker.org&#xA;                    &#xA;                  &#xA;                        " href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3098">72</a></sup> was used to generate species-specific repeat libraries for masking and annotation. Consensus repeat sequences with homology to known plant genes were removed from the repeat libraries. Homology was defined as having a significant (E-value &lt; 1 × 10<sup>−5</sup>) blastx<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Camacho, C. et al. BLAST+: architecture and applications. BMC Bioinformatics 10, 421 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3104">73</a></sup> hit to a subset of the PlantTribes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Wall, P. K. et al. PlantTribes: a gene and gene family resource for comparative genomics in plants. Nucleic Acids Res. 36, D970–D976 (2008)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3108">74</a></sup> v1.1 database that does not contain transposable element-related terms. Filtered RepeatModeler libraries were annotated with the name of the highest-scoring significant Repbase<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Bao, W., Kojima, K. K. &amp; Kohany, O. Repbase Update, a database of repetitive elements in eukaryotic genomes. Mob. DNA 6, 11 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3112">75</a></sup> v22.04 full database sequence (tblastx<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Camacho, C. et al. BLAST+: architecture and applications. BMC Bioinformatics 10, 421 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3117">73</a></sup>, E-value &lt; 1 × 10<sup>−5</sup>) and the highest-scoring significant Dfam<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Hubley, R. et al. The Dfam database of repetitive DNA families. Nucleic Acids Res. 44, D81–D89 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3123">76</a></sup> v2.0 profile hidden Markov model (HMM) (hmmsearch<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Eddy, S. R. Accelerated profile HMM searches. PLoS Comput. Biol. 7, e1002195 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3127">77</a></sup>, E-value &lt; 1 × 10<sup>−5</sup>).</p><p>LTR-RTs were discovered using structural criteria by the GenomeTools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Gremme, G., Steinbiss, S. &amp; Kurtz, S. GenomeTools: a comprehensive software library for efficient processing of structured genome annotations. IEEE/ACM Trans. Comput. Biol. Bioinform. 10, 645–656 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3136">78</a></sup> program LTRHarvest<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Ellinghaus, D., Kurtz, S. &amp; Willhoeft, U. LTRharvest, an efficient and flexible software for de novo detection of LTR retrotransposons. BMC Bioinformatics 9, 18 (2008)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3140">79</a></sup> with the following modifications to the default settings: a LTR similarity threshold of 0.01, an allowed LTR length range of 100–6,000 bp, an allowed distance between LTRs of a single element range of 1,000–25,000 bp and the number of bases outside LTR boundaries to search for target-site duplications set to 10. The GenomeTools program LTRDigest<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Steinbiss, S., Willhoeft, U., Gremme, G. &amp; Kurtz, S. Fine-grained annotation and classification of de novo predicted LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 37, 7002–7013 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3144">80</a></sup> was used with a set of 138 transposable element-related Pfam profile HMMs to annotate protein-coding domains in the internal regions of LTR-RTs.</p><p>We used 38 previously published <i>A.</i> <i>filiculoides</i> RNA-seq libraries and 6 <i>S. cucullata</i> libraries (see above) to assemble transcriptomes for facilitating gene model predictions. Reads from <i>A.</i> <i>filiculoides</i> and <i>S.</i> <i>cucullata</i> libraries were processed using a combination of Scythe<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Buffalo, V. Scythe—a Bayesian adapter trimmer v.0.994 beta (2014); &#xA;                    https://github.com/vsbuffalo/scythe&#xA;                    &#xA;                  &#xA;                        " href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3173">81</a></sup> and Sickle<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Joshi, N. A. &amp; Fass, J. N. Sickle: a sliding-window, adaptive, quality-based trimming tool for FastQ files v.1.33 (2011); &#xA;                    https://github.com/najoshi/sickle&#xA;                    &#xA;                  &#xA;                        " href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3177">82</a></sup> or SOAPnuke<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Chen, Y. et al. SOAPnuke: a MapReduce acceleration supported software for integrated quality control and preprocessing of high-throughput sequencing data. GigaScience 7, 1–6 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3181">83</a></sup>, with adapter and contaminating sequences discovered using FastQC<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Andrews, S. FastQC: a quality control tool for high throughput sequence data v.0.11.7 (Babraham Institute, 2018); &#xA;                    http://www.bioinformatics.babraham.ac.uk/projects/fastqc&#xA;                    &#xA;                  &#xA;                        " href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3185">84</a></sup> (v0.11.5). Approximately 627 million (<i>A.</i> <i>filiculoides</i>) and 259 million (<i>S.</i> <i>cucullata</i>) cleaned paired reads went into the assemblies. Libraries from experimental replicates were combined and assembled de novo by Trinity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 85" title="Grabherr, M. G. et al. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-seq data without a reference genome. Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 644–652 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3202">85</a></sup> (v2.0.6) and in a reference-guided manner using HISAT2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Kim, D., Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. HISAT: a fast spliced aligner with low memory requirements. Nat. Methods 12, 357–360 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3206">71</a></sup> (v2.0.4) and StringTie<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 86" title="Pertea, M. et al. StringTie enables improved reconstruction of a transcriptome from RNA-seq reads. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 290–295 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR86" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3211">86</a></sup> (v1.2.2), except for nine libraries published in de Vries et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="de Vries, J. et al. Cytokinin-induced promotion of root meristem size in the fern Azolla supports a shoot-like origin of euphyllophyte roots. New Phytol. 209, 705–720 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3215">59</a></sup> for which only a reference-guided approach was used. All programs used default parameters, and Trinity was run with the additional --trimmomatic option. StringTie results were merged using StringTie --merge, combined with the Trinity output, and were purged of redundant sequences using the GenomeTools sequniq utility<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Gremme, G., Steinbiss, S. &amp; Kurtz, S. GenomeTools: a comprehensive software library for efficient processing of structured genome annotations. IEEE/ACM Trans. Comput. Biol. Bioinform. 10, 645–656 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3219">78</a></sup>.</p><p>Putative centromere sequences were first identified by searching the genome assemblies with Tandem Repeat Finder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 87" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 573–580 1999)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR87" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3226">87</a></sup> to identify very high-copy (&gt;100 repeats) tandem repeats with a motif length in the range of 185–195 bp. Motif sequences were extracted from the Tandem Repeat Finder output and clustered using USEARCH<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 88" title="Edgar, R. C. Search and clustering orders of magnitude faster than BLAST. Bioinformatics 26, 2460–2461 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR88" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3230">88</a></sup>. A single major cluster was identified for each species and the sequences were separately aligned using MAFFT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Katoh, K., Misawa, K., Kuma, K.-I. &amp; Miyata, T. MAFFT: a novel method for rapid multiple sequence alignment based on fast Fourier transform. Nucleic Acids Res. 30, 3059–3066 (2002)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3234">89</a></sup>. Multiple sequence alignments for each species were used to generate a profile HMM representing the putative centromere sequences. We next used hmmsearch<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Eddy, S. R. Accelerated profile HMM searches. PLoS Comput. Biol. 7, e1002195 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3238">77</a></sup> to search the genome assemblies again to identify all regions with similarity to the centromere profile HMMs. Genomic regions with significant HMM matches were identified and these regions were annotated in a GFF3 format.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec14">Gene prediction</h3><p>Protein-coding genes were predicted using MAKER-P<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 90" title="Campbell, M. S. et al. MAKER-P: a tool kit for the rapid creation, management, and quality control of plant genome annotations. Plant Physiol. 164, 513–524 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR90" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3251">90</a></sup> (v2.31.8), and three MAKER-P iterations were performed: (1) repeat masking and creation of initial gene models from transcript and homologous protein evidence; (2) refinement of initial models with SNAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 91" title="Korf, I. Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinformatics 5, 59 (2004)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR91" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3255">91</a></sup> ab initio gene predictor trained on initial models; and (3) final models generated using SNAP and the ab initio gene predictor AUGUSTUS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 92" title="Stanke, M. &amp; Morgenstern, B. AUGUSTUS: a web server for gene prediction in eukaryotes that allows user-defined constraints. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, W465–W467 (2005)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR92" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3259">92</a></sup> trained on gene models from the second iteration.</p><p>Masking was performed by RepeatMasker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 93" title="Smit, A., Hubley, R. &amp; Green, P. RepeatMasker Open 4.0 (Institute for Systems Biology, 2015); &#xA;                    http://www.repeatmasker.org&#xA;                    &#xA;                  &#xA;                        " href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR93" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3266">93</a></sup> (v4.0.5) using the previously described species-specific repeat libraries and the full Repbase v22.04 database. After masking, gene models were inferred from transcripts and homologous protein sequences by first aligning to the genomes using BLAST+<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Camacho, C. et al. BLAST+: architecture and applications. BMC Bioinformatics 10, 421 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3270">73</a></sup> (v2.2.31) blastn or blastp, and then refined using the functions est2genome and protein2genome from the splice-site aware alignment program Exonerate<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 94" title="Slater, G. S. C. &amp; Birney, E. Automated generation of heuristics for biological sequence comparison. BMC Bioinformatics 6, 31 (2005)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR94" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3274">94</a></sup> (v2.2.0). We included the previously described <i>A.</i> <i>filiculoides</i> or <i>S.</i> <i>cucullata</i> transcriptomes and the set of protein sequences consisting of the full Swiss-prot database (downloaded 18 June 2016), <i>Amborella trichopoda</i> v1.0 proteins, <i>A.</i> <i>thaliana</i> TAIR10 proteins, <i>Chlamydomonas reinhardtii</i> v5.5 proteins, <i>Oryza sativa</i> v7.0 proteins and <i>Physcomitrella patens</i> v3.3 proteins (from Phytozome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 95" title="Goodstein, D. M. et al. Phytozome: a comparative platform for green plant genomics. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, D1178–D1186 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR95" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3310">95</a></sup>). Gene models with an annotation edit distance (AED) score of &lt;0.2 were used to train SNAP, which was used during the second iteration of MAKER-P. SNAP was retrained for the final iteration using gene models from the second iteration with an AED score of &lt;0.2 and a translated protein length of &gt;200 amino acids. Prior to training AUGUSTUS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 92" title="Stanke, M. &amp; Morgenstern, B. AUGUSTUS: a web server for gene prediction in eukaryotes that allows user-defined constraints. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, W465–W467 (2005)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR92" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3314">92</a></sup>, redundant sequences, defined as those sharing ≥70% sequence similarity in significant (E-value &lt; 1 × 10<sup>−5</sup>) HSPs from an all-by-all blastn alignment, were removed from the training set. Final non-redundant sets of 5,013 (<i>A.</i> <i>filiculoides</i>) or 6,475 (<i>S.</i> <i>cucullata</i>) gene models were used to train AUGUSTUS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 92" title="Stanke, M. &amp; Morgenstern, B. AUGUSTUS: a web server for gene prediction in eukaryotes that allows user-defined constraints. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, W465–W467 (2005)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR92" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3333">92</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec15">Phylogenomic inference and placement of WGDs from nuclear gene trees</h3><p>To infer ancient WGDs, we used a gene-tree sorting and counting algorithm, implemented in the MAPS tool<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Li, Z. et al. Early genome duplications in conifers and other seed plants. Sci. Adv. 1, e1501084 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3345">20</a></sup>. We selected four species of heterosporous ferns (two <i>Azolla</i>, one <i>Salvinia</i> and one <i>Pilularia</i>) and representatives of three other leptosporangiate lineages (<i>Blechnum</i>, <i>Lygodium</i> and <i>Dipteris</i>). The MAPS algorithm uses a given species tree to filter collections of nuclear gene trees for subtrees consistent with relationships at each node in the species tree. Using this filtered set of subtrees, MAPS identifies and records nodes with a gene duplication shared by descendant taxa. To infer and locate a potential WGD, we plotted the percentage of gene duplications shared by descendant taxa by node: a WGD will produce a large burst of shared duplications, appearing as an increase in the percentage of shared gene duplications<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Li, Z. et al. Early genome duplications in conifers and other seed plants. Sci. Adv. 1, e1501084 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3368">20</a></sup>.</p><p>We circumscribed and constructed nuclear gene family phylogenies from multiple species for each MAPS analysis. We translated each transcriptome into amino acid sequences using the TransPipe pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 96" title="Barker, M. S. et al. EvoPipes.net: bioinformatic tools for ecological and evolutionary genomics. Evol. Bioinformatics 6, 143–149 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR96" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3375">96</a></sup>. Using these translations, we performed reciprocal protein BLAST (blastp) searches among data sets for each MAPS analysis using an E-value cut-off of 10<sup>−5</sup>. We clustered gene families from these BLAST results using OrthoFinder with the default parameters<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 97" title="Emms, D. M. &amp; Kelly, S. OrthoFinder: solving fundamental biases in whole genome comparisons dramatically improves orthogroup inference accuracy. Genome Biol. 16, 157 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR97" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3381">97</a></sup> and only kept gene families that contained at least one gene copy from each taxon in a given MAPS analysis. We discarded the remaining OrthoFinder clusters. We used PASTA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 98" title="Mirarab, S. et al. PASTA: ultra-large multiple sequence alignment for nucleotide and amino-acid sequences. J. Comput. Biol. 22, 377–386 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR98" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3385">98</a></sup> for automatic alignment and phylogenetic reconstruction of gene families, employing MAFFT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Katoh, K., Misawa, K., Kuma, K.-I. &amp; Miyata, T. MAFFT: a novel method for rapid multiple sequence alignment based on fast Fourier transform. Nucleic Acids Res. 30, 3059–3066 (2002)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3389">89</a></sup> for constructing alignments, MUSCLE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 99" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792–1797 (2004)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR99" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3394">99</a></sup> for merging alignments and RAxML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 100" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML-VI-HPC: maximum likelihood-based phylogenetic analyses with thousands of taxa and mixed models. Bioinformatics 22, 2688–2690 (2006)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR100" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3398">100</a></sup> for tree estimation. The parameters for each software package were the default options for PASTA. For each gene family phylogeny, we ran PASTA until we reached three iterations without an improvement in the likelihood score using a centroid breaking strategy. We used the best-scoring PASTA tree for each multi-species nuclear gene family to infer and locate WGDs using MAPS.</p><p>For the null simulations, we first estimated the mean background gene duplication rate (<i>λ</i>) and the gene loss rate (<i>μ</i>) with WGDgc<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 101" title="Rabier, C.-E., Ta, T. &amp; Ane, C. Detecting and locating whole genome duplications on a phylogeny: a probabilistic approach. Mol. Biol. Evol. 31, 750–762 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR101" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3411">101</a></sup>. Gene count data were obtained from OrthoFinder clusters associated with each species tree. <i>λ</i> = 0.0031 and <i>μ</i> = 0.0039 were estimated using only gene clusters that spanned the root of their respective species trees, which has been shown to reduce biases in the maximum likelihood estimates of <i>λ</i> and <i>μ</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 101" title="Rabier, C.-E., Ta, T. &amp; Ane, C. Detecting and locating whole genome duplications on a phylogeny: a probabilistic approach. Mol. Biol. Evol. 31, 750–762 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR101" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3427">101</a></sup>. We chose a maximum gene family size of 100 for parameter estimation, which was necessary to provide an upper bound for numerical integration of node states<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 101" title="Rabier, C.-E., Ta, T. &amp; Ane, C. Detecting and locating whole genome duplications on a phylogeny: a probabilistic approach. Mol. Biol. Evol. 31, 750–762 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR101" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3431">101</a></sup>. We provided a prior probability distribution of 1.5 on the number of genes at the root of each species tree, such that ancestral gene family sizes followed a shifted geometric distribution with a mean equal to the average number of genes per gene family across species.</p><p>Gene trees were then simulated within each MAPS species tree using the GuestTreeGen program from GenPhyloData<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 102" title="Sjöstrand, J., Arvestad, L., Lagergren, J. &amp; Sennblad, B. GenPhyloData: realistic simulation of gene family evolution. BMC Bioinformatics 14, 209 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR102" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3438">102</a></sup>. We developed ultrametric species trees from the topological relationships inferred by the 1KP Consortium analyses and median branch lengths from TimeTree<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 103" title="Kumar, S. &amp; Hedges, S. B. TimeTree2: species divergence times on the iPhone. Bioinformatics 27, 2023–2024 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR103" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3442">103</a></sup>. For each species tree, we simulated 4,000 gene trees with at least one tip per species: 1,000 gene trees at the <i>λ</i> and <i>μ</i> maximum likelihood estimates, 1,000 gene trees at half the estimated <i>λ</i> and <i>μ</i>, 1,000 trees at three times <i>λ</i> and <i>μ</i>, and 1,000 trees at five times <i>λ</i> and <i>μ</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec16">Classification of syntenic duplicates and microsynteny analysis</h3><p>To distinguish gene duplicates as syntenic or tandem, we used the SynMap<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 104" title="Lyons, E., Pedersen, B., Kane, J. &amp; Freeling, M. The value of nonmodel genomes and an example using SynMap within CoGe to dissect the hexaploidy that predates the rosids. Trop. Plant Biol. 1, 181–190 (2008)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR104" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3479">104</a></sup> tool from the CoGe<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 105" title="Lyons, E. &amp; Freeling, M. How to usefully compare homologous plant genes and chromosomes as DNA sequences. Plant J. 53, 661–673 (2008)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR105" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3483">105</a></sup> platform, with default parameters and the Quota Align algorithm to merge syntenic blocks. Sets of syntenic paralogues or orthologues (defined by a collinear series of putative homologous genes) were extracted using the DAGChainer algorithm, whereas duplicates within ten genes apart in the same genomic region were identified as tandem duplicates (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM1">Supplementary Discussion</a>). Results for within <i>Azolla</i> and <i>Salvinia</i> genome comparisons, as well as between <i>Azolla</i> and <i>Salvinia</i>, can be regenerated using the links <a href="https://genomevolution.org/r/tozk">https://genomevolution.org/r/tozk</a>, <a href="https://genomevolution.org/r/toz7">https://genomevolution.org/r/toz7</a> and <a href="https://genomevolution.org/r/toyy">https://genomevolution.org/r/toyy</a>, respectively. Microsynteny analyses were performed using the GEvo tool from CoGe<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 105" title="Lyons, E. &amp; Freeling, M. How to usefully compare homologous plant genes and chromosomes as DNA sequences. Plant J. 53, 661–673 (2008)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR105" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3524">105</a></sup>. We used the default setting to define the minimum number of collinear genes for two regions to be called syntenic. Non-coding regions were masked in the two genomes to include only the protein-coding sequences. The two example microsyntenies shown in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#Fig5">5c</a> can be regenerated at <a href="https://genomevolution.org/r/ujll">https://genomevolution.org/r/ujll</a> and <a href="https://genomevolution.org/r/ukys">https://genomevolution.org/r/ukys</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec17">Gaussian mixture model analysis of <i>K</i>
                           <sub>s</sub> distributions</h3><p>Estimates of <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> were obtained for all pairs of syntenic paralogous and orthologous genes using the CODEML program<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 106" title="Goldman, N. &amp; Yang, Z. A codon-based model of nucleotide substitution for protein-coding DNA sequences. Mol. Biol. Evol. 11, 725–736 (1994)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR106" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3564">106</a></sup> in the PAML package (v4.8)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 107" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586–1591 (2007)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR107" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3568">107</a></sup> on the basis of codon sequence alignments. We used the GY model with stationary codon frequencies empirically estimated by the F3 × 4 model. Codon sequences were aligned with PRANK (version 100701) using the empirical codon model<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 108" title="Kosiol, C., Holmes, I. &amp; Goldman, N. An empirical codon model for protein sequence evolution. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1464–1479 (2007)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR108" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3572">108</a></sup> (setting -codon) to align coding DNA, always skipping insertions (-F). Only gene pairs with <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> values in the range of 0.05–5 were considered for further analyses. Gaussian mixture models were fitted to the resulting frequency distributions of <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> values by means of the densityMclust function in the R mclust version 5.3 package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 109" title="Scrucca, L., Fop, M., Murphy, T. B. &amp; Raftery, A. E. mclust 5: Clustering, classification and density estimation using Gaussian finite mixture models. R J. 8, 289–317 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR109" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3585">109</a></sup>. The Bayesian information criterion was used to determine the best-fitting model for the data, including the optimal number of Gaussian components to a maximum of nine. For each component, several parameters were computed including the mean and the variance, as well as the density mixing probabilities and the total number of gene pairs.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec18">Gene family classification and ancestral reconstruction</h3><p>The OrthoFinder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 97" title="Emms, D. M. &amp; Kelly, S. OrthoFinder: solving fundamental biases in whole genome comparisons dramatically improves orthogroup inference accuracy. Genome Biol. 16, 157 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR97" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3597">97</a></sup> clustering method was used to classify complete proteomes of 23 sequenced green plant genomes, including <i>A.</i> <i>filiculoides</i> and <i>S.</i> <i>cucullata</i> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM3">5</a>), into orthologous gene lineages (that is, orthogroups). We selected taxa that represented all of the major land plant and green algal lineages, including six core eudicots (<i>A.</i> <i>thaliana</i>, <i>Lotus japonicus</i>, <i>Populus trichocarpa</i>, <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i>, <i>Erythranthe guttata</i> and <i>Vitis vinifera</i>), five monocots (<i>O.</i> <i>sativa</i>, <i>Sorghum bicolor</i>, <i>Musa acuminata</i>, <i>Zostera marina</i> and <i>Spirodella polyrhiza</i>), one basal angiosperm (<i>A.</i> <i>trichopoda</i>), two gymnosperms (<i>Pinus taeda</i> and <i>Picea abies</i>), two ferns (<i>A.</i> <i>filiculoides</i> and <i>S.</i> <i>cucullata</i>), one lycophyte (<i>S.</i> <i>moellendorffii</i>), four bryophytes (<i>Sphagnum fallax</i>, <i>P.</i> <i>patens</i>, <i>Marchantia polymorpha</i> and <i>Jungermannia infusca</i>) and two green algae (<i>Klebsormidium flaccidum</i> and <i>C.</i> <i>reinhardtii</i>). In total, 16,817 orthogroups containing at least two genes were circumscribed, 8,680 of which contain at least one gene from either <i>A.</i> <i>filiculoides</i> or <i>S.</i> <i>cucullata</i>. Of the 20,203 annotated <i>A.</i> <i>filiculoides</i> genes and the 19,780 annotated <i>S.</i> <i>cucullata</i> genes, 17,941 (89%) and 16,807 (84%) were classified into orthogroups, respectively. The details for each orthogroup, including gene counts, secondary clustering of orthogroups (that is, super-orthogroups)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 110" title="Enright, A. J., Van Dongen, S. &amp; Ouzounis, C. A. An efficient algorithm for large-scale detection of protein families. Nucleic Acids Res. 30, 1575–1584 (2002)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR110" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3740">110</a></sup> and functional annotations, are reported in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM3">5</a>.</p><p>We used Wagner parsimony implemented in the program Count<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 111" title="Csurös, M. Count: evolutionary analysis of phylogenetic profiles with parsimony and likelihood. Bioinformatics 26, 1910–1912 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR111" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3750">111</a></sup> with a weighted gene gain penalty of 1.2 to reconstruct the ancestral gene content at key nodes in the phylogeny of the 23 land plants and green algae species (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM3">5</a>). The ancestral gene content dynamics—gains, losses, expansions and contractions—are depicted in Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM1">5</a>. Complete details of orthogroup dynamics for the key ancestral nodes that include seed plants, such as Salviniaceae, euphyllophytes and vascular plants, are reported in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM3">5</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec19">Transcription-associated protein characterization</h3><p>Transcription-associated proteins comprise transcription factors that bind in a sequence-specific manner to <i>cis</i>-regulatory DNA elements and transcriptional regulators that act via protein–protein interaction or chromatin modification. We conducted genome-wide, domain-based annotation of transcription-associated proteins according to previous studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Wilhelmsson, P. K. I., Mühlich, C., Ullrich, K. K. &amp; Rensing, S. A. Comprehensive genome-wide classification reveals that many plant-specific transcription factors evolved in streptophyte algae. Genome Biol. Evol. 9, 3384–3397 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3774">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 112" title="Lang, D. et al. Genome-wide phylogenetic comparative analysis of plant transcriptional regulation: a timeline of loss, gain, expansion, and correlation with complexity. Genome Biol. Evol. 2, 488–503 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR112" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3777">112</a></sup>. A total of 1,206 (6%, <i>Azolla</i>) and 983 (7%, <i>Salvinia</i>) proteins were sorted into families; this amount is similar to <i>Selaginella</i> but less than in gymnosperms or angiosperms (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM6">8</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec20">PPR annotation</h3><p>We conducted a targeted annotation for PPR genes because they are generally only weakly expressed and thus often lack transcriptome support. Open reading frames from the nuclear genome assemblies were translated into amino acid sequences using the “getorf” tool from the EMBOSS (v.6.5.7) package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 113" title="Rice, P., Longden, I. &amp; Bleasby, A. EMBOSS: the European Molecular Biology Open Software Suite. Trends Genet. 16, 276–277 (2000)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR113" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3803">113</a></sup> with a minimum size restriction of 300 nucleotides. These open reading frames were searched for PPR motifs using the hmmsearch tool from the HMMER3 package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Eddy, S. R. Accelerated profile HMM searches. PLoS Comput. Biol. 7, e1002195 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3807">77</a></sup>. The PPR motif models and parameters used follow those of Cheng et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Cheng, S. et al. Redefining the structural motifs that determine RNA binding and RNA editing by pentatricopeptide repeat proteins in land plants. Plant J. 85, 532–547 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3811">27</a></sup>. Motifs were assembled into full PPR tracts and the best model for each PPR was determined<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Cheng, S. et al. Redefining the structural motifs that determine RNA binding and RNA editing by pentatricopeptide repeat proteins in land plants. Plant J. 85, 532–547 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3815">27</a></sup>.</p><p>To study the prevalence and location of RNA editing, non-poly(A)-enriched RNA-seq data were filtered to remove adapters, low-quality reads and reads with ≥5% Ns. Clean reads were aligned against the assembled plastid and mitochondrial genome assemblies using TopHat 2.0 (ref.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 114" title="Kim, D. et al. TopHat2: accurate alignment of transcriptomes in the presence of insertions, deletions and gene fusions. Genome Biol. 14, R36 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR114" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3822">114</a></sup>). One of the inverted repeat regions in the plastid genomes was removed before mapping. Only uniquely mapped reads were retained as input for SAMtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 115" title="Li, H. et al. The Sequence Alignment/Map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078–2079 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR115" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3826">115</a></sup> to call mismatches between RNA and the corresponding DNA. Differences between corresponding RNA and DNA sequences were identified as the putative RNA-editing sites. The RNA-editing level was defined as the number of altered reads divided by the total mapped reads for each site.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec21">Phylogeny of the insecticidal protein Tma12</h3><p>We used BLASTp<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Camacho, C. et al. BLAST+: architecture and applications. BMC Bioinformatics 10, 421 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3838">73</a></sup> to search for <i>Tma12</i> (Genbank accession: JQ438776) homologues in Phytozome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 95" title="Goodstein, D. M. et al. Phytozome: a comparative platform for green plant genomics. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, D1178–D1186 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR95" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3845">95</a></sup>, 1KP transcriptomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Matasci, N. et al. Data access for the 1,000 Plants (1KP) project. GigaScience 3, 1–10 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3849">32</a></sup> and the NCBI Genbank non-redundant protein database. Although <i>Tma12</i> homologues are present in fern transcriptomes and in the <i>S.</i> <i>cucullata</i> genome, no significant hit was found in any other plant genomes or transcriptomes. In addition, the majority of the Tma12 protein is composed of a chitin-binding domain that belongs to the PF03067 Pfam family. This family does not contain any plant genes but is predominantly represented in the genomes of Actinobacteria, insects and fungi. To trace the origin of fern <i>Tma12</i> genes, we downloaded representative sequences containing PF03067 and PF08329 (as the outgroup) from UniProt and Genbank and reconstructed the phylogeny using IQ-TREE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 116" title="Nguyen, L.-T., Schmidt, H. A., von Haeseler, A. &amp; Minh, B. Q. IQ-TREE: a fast and effective stochastic algorithm for estimating maximum-likelihood phylogenies. Mol. Biol. Evol. 32, 268–274 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR116" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3866">116</a></sup>. We then used this preliminary phylogeny (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM1">Supplementary Data</a>) to construct a more focused data set to narrow down the phylogenetic affinity of <i>Tma12</i>. PartitionFinder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 117" title="Lanfear, R., Calcott, B., Ho, S. Y. W. &amp; Guindon, S. Partitionfinder: combined selection of partitioning schemes and substitution models for phylogenetic analyses. Mol. Biol. Evol. 29, 1695–1701 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR117" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3877">117</a></sup> was used to infer the optimal codon partition scheme and substitution models, and RAxML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 100" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML-VI-HPC: maximum likelihood-based phylogenetic analyses with thousands of taxa and mixed models. Bioinformatics 22, 2688–2690 (2006)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR100" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3881">100</a></sup> was used for maximum likelihood phylogeny inference and to calculate bootstrap branch support.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec22">
                           <i>Azolla</i> phylogeny</h3><p>From the resequencing data (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM1">12</a>), we compiled both plastome and nuclear phylogenomic data sets to infer the <i>Azolla</i> species phylogeny. <i>S.</i> <i>cucullata</i> was used as the outgroup. For the plastome phylogeny, we concatenated nucleotide alignments from 83 protein-coding genes and used PartitionFinder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 117" title="Lanfear, R., Calcott, B., Ho, S. Y. W. &amp; Guindon, S. Partitionfinder: combined selection of partitioning schemes and substitution models for phylogenetic analyses. Mol. Biol. Evol. 29, 1695–1701 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR117" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3909">117</a></sup> to identify the optimal data partition scheme and the associated nucleotide substitution models. RAxML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 100" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML-VI-HPC: maximum likelihood-based phylogenetic analyses with thousands of taxa and mixed models. Bioinformatics 22, 2688–2690 (2006)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR100" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3914">100</a></sup> was used for maximum likelihood phylogeny inference and to calculate bootstrap branch support. For the nuclear data set, we focused on genes that, based on the gene family classification results, are single copy in both <i>A.</i> <i>filiculoides</i> and <i>S.</i> <i>cucullata</i> genomes. We used HybPiper<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 118" title="Johnson, M. G. et al. HybPiper: extracting coding sequence and introns for phylogenetics from high-throughput sequencing reads using target enrichment. Appl. Plant Sci. 4, 1600016 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR118" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3930">118</a></sup> to extract the exon sequences from each of the resequenced species. The ‘bwa’ option was used in HybPiper instead of the ‘blastx’ default. We then filtered out genes with more than two species missing or having an average sequence length shorter than 75% of the one in <i>A.</i> <i>filiculoides</i>. This resulted in a final data set of 2,108 genes. Sequence alignments and gene tree inferences were done in PASTA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 98" title="Mirarab, S. et al. PASTA: ultra-large multiple sequence alignment for nucleotide and amino-acid sequences. J. Comput. Biol. 22, 377–386 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR98" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3941">98</a></sup>, with the default setting, except that RAxML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 100" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML-VI-HPC: maximum likelihood-based phylogenetic analyses with thousands of taxa and mixed models. Bioinformatics 22, 2688–2690 (2006)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR100" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3945">100</a></sup> was used to estimate the best tree on the final alignment. To infer the species tree from these gene trees, we used the multi-species coalescent method implemented in ASTRAL-III (v5.6.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 119" title="Zhang, C., Sayyari, E. &amp; Mirarab, S. ASTRAL-III: polynomial time species tree reconstruction from partially resolved gene trees. BMC Evol. Biol. 19, 153 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR119" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3949">119</a></sup>. The tree topology from the plastome and nuclear data sets were identical, and all nodes received bootstrap support of 100 and a local posterior probability of 1.0.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec23">Cyanobiont phylogeny</h3><p>To compare the host and symbiont phylogenies, we assembled the cyanobiont genomes from five additional <i>Azolla</i> species (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM1">12</a>) using the resequencing data generated from total DNAs, including sequences derived from both the host and the cyanobiont. To extract the cyanobiont genomes from each of the <i>Azolla</i> species, we first filtered out chloroplast sequences by using BWA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows–Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754–1760 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3970">70</a></sup> (default parameters) to map the total clean DNA reads against each chloroplast genome reference. In this step, ~3–4% of the reads were filtered out, which is necessary to remove plastid ribosomal RNAs that are highly similar to ones in the cyanobionts. For each of the five <i>Azolla</i> species, we then mapped the filtered reads to the published cyanobiont reference (<i>N.</i> <i>azollae</i> 0708 isolated from <i>A.</i> <i>filiculoides</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Ran, L. et al. Genome erosion in a nitrogen-fixing vertically transmitted endosymbiotic multicellular cyanobacterium. PLoS ONE 5, e11486 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3989">11</a></sup>; Genbank accession: NC_014248) using BLAST<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Camacho, C. et al. BLAST+: architecture and applications. BMC Bioinformatics 10, 421 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d52717e3993">73</a></sup> (alignment criteria: E-value ≤ 1 × 10<sup>−5</sup>, sequence identity of ≥90% and an aligned coverage of ≥90%). Only the aligned reads were assembled by Mitobim<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Hahn, C., Bachmann, L. &amp; Chevreux, B. Reconstructing mitochondrial genomes directly from genomic next-generation sequencing reads—a baiting and iterative mapping approach. Nucleic Acids Res. 41, e129 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d52717e4000">64</a></sup> (iterations = 5) using <i>N.</i> <i>azollae</i> 0708 (ref.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Ran, L. et al. Genome erosion in a nitrogen-fixing vertically transmitted endosymbiotic multicellular cyanobacterium. PLoS ONE 5, e11486 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d52717e4010">11</a></sup>) as a reference. Gene prediction for each assembled cyanobiont was performed by the Prodigal program<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 120" title="Hyatt, D. et al. Prodigal: prokaryotic gene recognition and translation initiation site identification. BMC Bioinformatics 11, 119 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR120" id="ref-link-section-d52717e4014">120</a></sup>. Transfer RNAs were predicted by tRNAscan-SE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 121" title="Lowe, T. M. &amp; Eddy, S. R. tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 955–964 (1997)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR121" id="ref-link-section-d52717e4019">121</a></sup> using a bacterial tRNA gene structure model. The presence of rRNA sequences (gene number and structure) for each cyanobiont was confirmed by mapping the rRNAs of <i>N.</i> <i>azollae</i> 0708 against each assembled genome cyanobiont sequence using BLAST. We used mugsy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 122" title="Angiuoli, S. V. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Mugsy: fast multiple alignment of closely related whole genomes. Bioinformatics 27, 334–342 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR122" id="ref-link-section-d52717e4029">122</a></sup> to generate the whole-genome alignment, which resulted in a nucleotide matrix of 5,354,840 characters. IQ-TREE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 116" title="Nguyen, L.-T., Schmidt, H. A., von Haeseler, A. &amp; Minh, B. Q. IQ-TREE: a fast and effective stochastic algorithm for estimating maximum-likelihood phylogenies. Mol. Biol. Evol. 32, 268–274 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR116" id="ref-link-section-d52717e4033">116</a></sup> was used for model testing and maximum likelihood tree inference. Because the <i>N.</i> <i>azollae</i> genome is reduced in size and is significantly diverged from other cyanobacteria, we could not find an appropriate outgroup to root the cyanobiont tree. To overcome this, we used STRIDE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 123" title="Emms, D. M. &amp; Kelly, S. STRIDE: species tree root inference from gene duplication events. Mol. Biol. Evol. 34, 3267–3278 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR123" id="ref-link-section-d52717e4044">123</a></sup> implemented in OrthoFinder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 97" title="Emms, D. M. &amp; Kelly, S. OrthoFinder: solving fundamental biases in whole genome comparisons dramatically improves orthogroup inference accuracy. Genome Biol. 16, 157 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR97" id="ref-link-section-d52717e4048">97</a></sup> to locate the root by reconciling gene trees. STRIDE was run with the default setting, except that MAFFT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Katoh, K., Misawa, K., Kuma, K.-I. &amp; Miyata, T. MAFFT: a novel method for rapid multiple sequence alignment based on fast Fourier transform. Nucleic Acids Res. 30, 3059–3066 (2002)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d52717e4052">89</a></sup> was used for alignment and RAxML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 100" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML-VI-HPC: maximum likelihood-based phylogenetic analyses with thousands of taxa and mixed models. Bioinformatics 22, 2688–2690 (2006)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR100" id="ref-link-section-d52717e4056">100</a></sup> for tree inference. The root was found to be the node placing the <i>A.</i> <i>nilotica</i> cyanobiont as sister to a clade comprising all other cyanobionts. The reconciled species tree is identical to the tree reconstructed from the whole-genome alignment.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec24">Identification of the CSP genes</h3><p>The <i>Medicago truncatula DMI2</i>, <i>DMI3</i>, <i>IPD3</i>, <i>CASTER/POLLUX</i> and <i>VAPYRIN</i> sequences were used as queries, as in a previous study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Delaux, P.-M. et al. Algal ancestor of land plants was preadapted for symbiosis. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 112, 13390–13395 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d52717e4091">39</a></sup>, to search against the genomes and transcriptomes from species listed in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#MOESM9">11</a> using tBLASTn<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Camacho, C. et al. BLAST+: architecture and applications. BMC Bioinformatics 10, 421 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d52717e4098">73</a></sup>. For liverworts and ferns from the 1KP data set<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Matasci, N. et al. Data access for the 1,000 Plants (1KP) project. GigaScience 3, 1–10 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d52717e4102">32</a></sup>, non-annotated transcriptomes were used as targets, with the longest open reading frame of each contig extracted and translated. For <i>A.</i> <i>filiculoides</i> and <i>S.</i> <i>cucullata</i>, both the annotated gene models and the unannotated scaffolds were used. All hits that matched already annotated gene models were discarded prior to subsequent analyses. No homologues were identified in the two fern genomes for <i>IPD3</i> and <i>VAPYRIN</i>. Protein sequences for <i>DMI2</i>/<i>SYMRK</i>, <i>DMI3</i>/<i>CCaMK</i> and <i>CASTOR</i>/<i>POLLUX</i> were aligned using MAFFT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Katoh, K., Misawa, K., Kuma, K.-I. &amp; Miyata, T. MAFFT: a novel method for rapid multiple sequence alignment based on fast Fourier transform. Nucleic Acids Res. 30, 3059–3066 (2002)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d52717e4144">89</a></sup>. The best substitution model for each alignment (JTT for all alignments) was determined using MEGA6 (ref.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 124" title="Tamura, K., Stecher, G., Peterson, D., Filipski, A. &amp; Kumar, S. MEGA6: molecular evolutionary genetics analysis version 6.0. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 2725–2729 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR124" id="ref-link-section-d52717e4148">124</a></sup>). Phylogenetic trees were generated using RAxML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 100" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML-VI-HPC: maximum likelihood-based phylogenetic analyses with thousands of taxa and mixed models. Bioinformatics 22, 2688–2690 (2006)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR100" id="ref-link-section-d52717e4153">100</a></sup> on the CIPRES platform<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 125" title="Miller, M. A., Pfeiffer, W. &amp; Schwartz, T. Creating the CIPRES Science Gateway for inference of large phylogenetic trees. In 2010 Gateway Computing Environments Workshop 1–8 (IEEE, 2010); &#xA;                    https://doi.org/dc3c34&#xA;                    &#xA;                  &#xA;                        " href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR125" id="ref-link-section-d52717e4157">125</a></sup>, and node support was assessed with 100 rapid bootstrap pseudoreplicates.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec25">Quantitative real-time PCR of <i>nifH</i>
                        </h3><p>Quantitative real-time PCR for the <i>N.</i> <i>azollae nifH</i> gene was conducted using total RNA extracted from <i>A.</i> <i>filiculoides</i>. Primers were derived from Brouwer et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Brouwer, P. et al. Azolla domestication towards a biobased economy? New Phytol. 202, 1069–1082 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d52717e4185">58</a></sup>. ThermoFisher Superscript IV was used to generate complementary DNA from the RNA. The cDNA was then used for quantitative PCR with the Roche SYBR Green Master Mix on a Chromo4 real-time PCR machine with the Opticon platform. The relative gene expression was calculated using the 2<sup>ΔC(t)</sup> method, with the cyanobacteria present/nitrogen absent condition as the reference.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec26">
                           <i>Azolla</i> symbiosis transcriptome analysis</h3><p>We used RNA-seq to compare gene expression patterns of AzCy+ and AzCy− individuals grown with or without ammonium nitrate. Each condition and treatment combination has three biological replicates. RNA-seq reads were mapped to the <i>A.</i> <i>filiculoides</i> genome by HISAT2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Kim, D., Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. HISAT: a fast spliced aligner with low memory requirements. Nat. Methods 12, 357–360 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d52717e4211">71</a></sup>, and read counts for each gene were calculated using the HTSeq software package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 126" title="Anders, S., Pyl, P. T. &amp; Huber, W. HTSeq—a Python framework to work with high-throughput sequencing data. Bioinformatics 31, 166–169 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR126" id="ref-link-section-d52717e4215">126</a></sup>. We used the rlog function in the DESeq2 package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 127" title="Anders, S. &amp; Huber, W. Differential expression analysis for sequence count data. Genome Biol. 11, R106 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0188-8#ref-CR127" id="ref-link-section-d52717e4219">127</a></sup> for data normalization and carried out differential expression analysis in DESeq2 to identify upregulated and downregulated genes with an adjusted <i>P</i> value of 0.005. Distance clustering and principal component analysis were used to examine the relatedness of samples and conditions as a quality-control measure.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec27">
                           <i>Azolla</i>–cyanobacteria HGT</h3><p>To identify cyanobiont-derived genes in the <i>A.</i> <i>filiculoides</i> genome, we first investigated a potential orthologous relationship between any <i>Azolla</i> genes and cyanobacteria. For this, we used the <i>Azolla</i> genome assembly as a query for a DIAMOND BLASTx<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 128" title="Buchfink, B., Xie, C. &amp; Huson, D. H. Fast and sensitive protein alignment using DIAMOND. Nat.
